\"Solorigate\"'s Identity IOCs - for Identity Vendors and their customers.","id":"message:2007610","revisionNum":16,"repliesCount":2,"author":{"__ref":"User:user:221690"},"depth":0,"hasGivenKudo":false,"board":{"__ref":"Blog:board:microsoft-entra-blog"},"conversation":{"__ref":"Conversation:conversation:2007610"},"messagePolicies":{"__typename":"MessagePolicies","canPublishArticleOnEdit":{"__typename":"PolicyResult","failureReason":{"__typename":"FailureReason","message":"error.lithium.policies.forums.policy_can_publish_on_edit_workflow_action.accessDenied","key":"error.lithium.policies.forums.policy_can_publish_on_edit_workflow_action.accessDenied","args":[]}},"canModerateSpamMessage":{"__typename":"PolicyResult","failureReason":{"__typename":"FailureReason","message":"error.lithium.policies.feature.moderation_spam.action.moderate_entity.allowed.accessDenied","key":"error.lithium.policies.feature.moderation_spam.action.moderate_entity.allowed.accessDenied","args":[]}}},"contentWorkflow":{"__typename":"ContentWorkflow","state":"PUBLISH","scheduledPublishTime":null,"scheduledTimezone":null,"userContext":{"__typename":"MessageWorkflowContext","canSubmitForReview":null,"canEdit":false,"canRecall":null,"canSubmitForPublication":null,"canReturnToAuthor":null,"canPublish":null,"canReturnToReview":null,"canSchedule":false},"shortScheduledTimezone":null},"readOnly":false,"editFrozen":false,"moderationData":{"__ref":"ModerationData:moderation_data:2007610"},"teaser":"\u003cP\u003eIn this document, we are sharing what we know about the Solorigate (\u003cA href=\"https://aka.ms/solorigate\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003ehttps://aka.ms/solorigate\u003c/A\u003e)\u0026nbsp;attacks from an Identity specific lens, so Identity vendors, IAAS, PAAS, and SAAS vendors and organizations who use these products can detect, remediate, and most importantly, prevent these and similar attacks.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cspan class=\"lia-media-object lia-media-is-inline lia-media-size-large\" data-image-alt=\"Attack overview.png\"\u003e\u003cbutton class=\"lia-media-unstyled-btn\" type=\"button\" aria-haspopup=\"true\" aria-label=\"Enlarge Image Attack overview.png\"\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0yMDA3NjEwLTI0MjE1N2k5N0I1MTNDODkxMkExQ0FE?image-dimensions=832x481\u0026amp;revision=16\" width=\"832\" height=\"481\" alt=\"Attack overview.png\" /\u003e\u003c/button\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/P\u003e","body":"\u003cP\u003eMicrosoft recently \u003cA href=\"https://blogs.microsoft.com/on-the-issues/2020/12/13/customers-protect-nation-state-cyberattacks/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003edisclosed a set of complex techniques used by an advanced actor\u003c/A\u003e to execute attacks against several key customers. While we detected anomalies by analyzing requests from customer environments to the Microsoft 365 cloud, the attacks generalize to any Identity Provider or Service provider. For this reason, we want to detail what we know about these attacks from an Identity specific lens, so Identity vendors, IAAS, PAAS, and SAAS vendors (and organizations who use these products) can \u0026nbsp;detect them, remediate them, and most importantly, prevent them. This document is broadly intended to support the security teams who are focused on protection, hunting, and remediation in Identity vendors in the interest of protecting our mutual customers. It generally assumes an advanced understanding of identity infrastructure and related components.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eAs part of our ongoing security processes, we leverage threat intelligence and monitor for new indicators that could signal attacker activity. There are two anomalies pertinent to this report, and discussed at \u003cA href=\"https://aka.ms/solorigate\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003ehttps://aka.ms/solorigate\u003c/A\u003e:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cOL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAnomalies in SAML tokens being presented for access. In some impacted tenants, we detected anomalous SAML tokens - signed with customer certificates - being presented for access to the Microsoft Cloud. The anomalies indicate that the customer SAML token signing certificates may have been compromised, and that an attacker could be forging SAML tokens to access any resources that trust those certificates. Because compromise of a SAML token signing certificate typically requires administrative access, there is a high probability the presence of forged tokens implies customer on premises infrastructure may be compromised. Because the signing certificate is the root of trust for the federated trust relationship, it is unlikely Service Providers would detect the forgeries.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cSPAN\u003eAnomalies in Microsoft 365 API access patterns in a tenant. We have detected such anomalies in some impacted tenants which originate from an existing applications and service principals. This pattern indicates that an attacker with administrative credentials – possibly related to the SAML issues above - has added their own credentials to existing applications and service principals. The Microsoft 365 application program interfaces (APIs) can be used to access email, documents, chats, and configuration settings, such as email forwarding. Because highly privileged Azure AD administrative accounts are required to add credentials to service principals, these changes imply that one or more such privileged accounts have been compromised and may have made other significant changes within the impacted tenant.\u003c/SPAN\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/OL\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eBuilt-in security and monitoring capabilities provided by the Microsoft Cloud detected these anomalies, but such anomalies could present at any Identity Provider or Resource Provider, regardless of vendor.\u0026nbsp; Any resource which trusts a customer’s compromised SAML token signing certificate should be considered at risk.\u0026nbsp;The SAML attack is not specific to any particular identity system or identity vendor you use. It impacts any vendor’s on-premises or cloud identity system, and any resources that depend on industry-standard SAML identity federation. Likewise, while the specific mechanism for impersonating applications may vary from vendor to vendor, the pattern is vendor independent. We are not aware of any Microsoft software or cloud service vulnerability that has led to the exposure of customer SAML token signing certificates, the change in API usage, addition of credentials to service principals, or exposure of administrative credentials on premises or in the cloud. The traffic detected did not impact our production cloud environment\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eElements of the activity we have detected and discuss in this blog suggest it was orchestrated by a sophisticated attacker. We believe over time this activity may be determined to be state-sponsored, but we do not have sufficient evidence to support that conclusion at this time.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eIt is important to emphasize – these attacks were criminal in nature, and so sophisticated that even top security companies fell victim to them. The fault lies with the criminal actor, not the victims. Further, these attacks leveraged broadly used patterns that impact many levels of the IT industry – focusing on any one technology type or vendor limits our ability to see systemic problems. By studying them and learning from these broad issues together, we can improve security throughout the industry.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eWe are sharing this information to help vendors who provide Identity Providers and Service Provider software and services, as well as their customers, hunt for activity and better defend against similar attacks. We will update this information as our investigations evolve.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--557377520\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-1930135313\"\u003eFirst things first – understanding typical environments\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003eIdentity experts may skip this section, but it may help level set terminology and understanding of key components of the attack.\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe following describes a typical environment. However, several caveats are worth mentioning:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cOL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eMost customers run on premises identity infrastructure. Microsoft offers popular versions of all such infrastructure, but several other vendors offer similar infrastructure.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eNot all components of this infrastructure are present in all environments.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe sophisticated actor adapted their attack to specific environments.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/OL\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eFor these reasons, it is important that you consider how elements of the attack pattern can impact your organization, even if your organization varies from the typical elements described below.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eComponents pertinent to this attack typically include:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eA \u003cU\u003eSAML Identity Provider (IDP)\u003c/U\u003e, which brokers authentication requests from applications to that directory. Applications and other identity providers – called Service Providers, or SPs – refer clients to federation servers to acquire access tokens which are signed by the SAML token signing certificate.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cU\u003eSAML Service Providers (SP)\u003c/U\u003e, also called Relying Parties, which are applications told to trust the tokens coming from a federation server, and which accept claims made in SAML tokens because they are signed by the trusted federation server’s SAML token signing certificate.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cU\u003eCloud Identity Providers (Cloud IDPs)\u003c/U\u003e, which would typically act as a Service Provider to the SAML Identity Provider but acts as an identity provider to applications which have been told to use it. (This is called Identity Provider chaining – where one Identity provider delegates authentication to another if so configured). Azure AD is an example of a Cloud IDP.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cU\u003eCloud Service Providers (Cloud SPs)\u003c/U\u003e, which rely on the tokens issued by a cloud IDP to validate requests.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cU\u003eApplications\u003c/U\u003e and \u003cU\u003eService Principals\u003c/U\u003e in Azure AD are two types of code that authenticate to Azure AD using certificates, keys, or passwords and to other applications or APIs using OAuth 2.0 access tokens granted Azure AD in order to talk to other applications, including Microsoft 365 Graph APIs.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cDIV id=\"tinyMceEditorAlex Weinert_0\" class=\"mceNonEditable lia-copypaste-placeholder\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/DIV\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cspan class=\"lia-media-object lia-media-is-inline lia-media-size-large\" data-image-alt=\"Components.png\"\u003e\u003cbutton class=\"lia-media-unstyled-btn\" type=\"button\" aria-haspopup=\"true\" aria-label=\"Enlarge Image Components.png\"\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0yMDA3NjEwLTI0MjE1NGlCODZCMEJDRkVFMTNFOTA3?image-dimensions=672x277\u0026amp;revision=16\" width=\"672\" height=\"277\" alt=\"Components.png\" /\u003e\u003c/button\u003e\u003cspan class=\"lia-media-caption-text\"\u003eTypical Components\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eIf this is new to you, you can think of tokens as a ticket to a show, the SP as the ticket taker, and the IDP as the box office, and the signature as the little holograph on the ticket. If someone wants to go to the show, they go to the IDP, get a token, then present it to the SP who validates the holograph then lets them into the show.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eIn terms of a typical email interaction with Microsoft 365 with federated auth, think in terms of a user going to their on premises federation server to get a signed token, then presenting that token to Microsoft 365 (via Azure AD as a chained IDP) to get access after the signature is validated. If an application is trying to access that data, it will be pre-configured to know the IDP location, so it will always come to Azure AD first to get the token, but otherwise the OAUTH flows are similar.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eFor SAML federation relationships where Azure AD has been configured to trust a tenant-configured SAML token signing certificate from a customer-configured federation server, the federation server is the Identity Provider (IDP)and Azure AD is the Service Provider (SP).\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eApplications and Service Principals in Azure AD don’t use SAML or delegate trust to on premises federation servers; credentials must be managed directly in Azure AD.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-122680850\"\u003eAttacker Patterns\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eOK, let’s lay all of this out in terms of the typical attack patterns, and how you might go about looking for them. The actions were targeted in nature and varied from target to target. Not all indicators of compromise or methodologies are present in all cases.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eA rough overview of an indicative attack is as follows (see also \u003cA href=\"https://aka.ms/solorigate\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003ehttps://aka.ms/solorigate\u003c/A\u003e). Each attack varied in details, but this pattern emerged repeatedly:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe attacker compromises network management vendor software which is typically deployed with high privileges in on premises networks.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe attacker uses the on premises administrative access to extract SAML Token Signing certificate.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe attacker uses the stolen SAML Token Signing certificate to forge SAML tokens representing privileged cloud users.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe attacker uses the forged token to sign in impersonating the privileged user and add credentials to an existing application or service principal.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe attacker uses the added credentials to impersonate the existing application or service principal, and call Microsoft 365 APIs to extract mail.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cDIV id=\"tinyMceEditorAlex Weinert_1\" class=\"mceNonEditable lia-copypaste-placeholder\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/DIV\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cspan class=\"lia-media-object lia-media-is-inline lia-media-size-large\" data-image-alt=\"Attack overview.png\"\u003e\u003cbutton class=\"lia-media-unstyled-btn\" type=\"button\" aria-haspopup=\"true\" aria-label=\"Enlarge Image Attack overview.png\"\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0yMDA3NjEwLTI0MjE1NmlERUU4OEJGQkI2RkMzMDlE?image-dimensions=832x481\u0026amp;revision=16\" width=\"832\" height=\"481\" alt=\"Attack overview.png\" /\u003e\u003c/button\u003e\u003cspan class=\"lia-media-caption-text\"\u003eAttacker Patterns\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe details of each phase of the attack are described below.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--1684773613\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-802739220\"\u003ePattern 1: Forged SAML tokens using Stolen SAML Token Signing Material\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe first significant pattern we have seen is evidence of forged SAML tokens. It is worth noting that we don’t retain logs for very long. We do this in accordance with our data retention and privacy policies. It varies by agreement, but is generally is 30 days, and we never log complete tokens, so we can’t see every aspect of a SAML token. Customers who want longer retention are encouraged to configure storage in Azure Monitor or other systems. What token anomalies we did detect were tokens which were anomalous in lifetime, usage location, or claims (particularly MFA claims). The anomalies were sufficient to convince us that the tokens were forged. We did not find this pattern in all cases.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-1493300694\"\u003eWhat we found\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTokens with expiration instant exactly 3600 seconds or 144000 seconds (no milliseconds value) – note 144000 (40 hours) is exceptionally long\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTokens which were received at the same time as the issuance time– no latency between creation and usage\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTokens which were received BEFORE the issuance time – falsified issuance time after token received\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTokens used from outside normal user locations\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTokens which contained claims which were not previously seen from the tenant’s federation server\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTokens indicating MFA used when token claimed auth from within corporate boundary where MFA not required\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--314153769\"\u003eWhat it implies\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eSAML token signing certificate was exfiltrated from the customer environment and used to forge tokens by the actor.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAdministrative access to SAML Token Signing Certificate storage compromised, either via service administrative access or by direct device storage or memory inspection.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eDeep penetration of the customer environment, with administrative access to identity infrastructure or the hardware environment on which it executes.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--2121608232\"\u003eWhat to look for\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eSAML Tokens received by the SP with configurations which deviate from the IDP’s configured behavior.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eSAML Tokens received by the SP without corresponding issuing logs at the IDP.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eSAML Tokens received by the SP with MFA claims but without corresponding MFA activity logs at the IDP.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eSAML Tokens which are received from IP addresses, agents, times, or for services which are anomalous for the requesting identity represented in the token.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eEvidence of unauthorized administrative activity.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-365904601\"\u003eWhat to do\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eDetermine mechanism of certificate exfiltration and remediate (see below)\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eRoll all SAML token signing certificates\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eConsider reducing your reliance on prem SAML trust where possible\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eConsider using an HSM to manage your SAML Token Signing Certificates\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-355401497\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-221716773\"\u003ePattern 2: Illegitimate registrations of SAML Trust Relationships\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eIn some cases, the SAML token forgeries described above correspond to configuration changes in the Service Provider. By impersonating a user with valid administrative credentials, the actor can change the configuration of the SAML Service Provider (in our case, Azure AD). In this case, the actor tells Azure AD to trust their certificate by, in effect, saying to the SP “There’s another SAML IDP you should trust, validate it with this public key.”\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--1456654971\"\u003eWhat we found\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAddition of federation trust relationships at the SP done which later resulted in SAML authentications of users with administrative privileges. The attacker took care to follow naming conventions of or impersonate existing federation server names (e.g. FED_SERVER01 exists, and they add FED_SERVERO1). The impersonated users later took actions consistent with attacker patterns described below.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eToken forgeries consistent with pattern 1, above.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThese calls came from different IP addresses for each call and user, but generally tracked back to anonymizing VPN servers.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-1030857862\"\u003eWhat it implies\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAdministrative access to the Azure AD was gained.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAttacker may have been unable to gain a toe-hold on premises, or was experimenting with other persistence mechanisms.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAttacker may have been unable to exfiltrate tokens, possibly due to use of HSM.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--776596601\"\u003eWhat to look for\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAnomalous administrative session associated with modification of federation trust relationships.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-1710916232\"\u003eWhat to do\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eReview all federation trust relationships, ensure all are valid.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eDetermine mechanism of administrative account impersonation (see below).\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eRoll administrative account credentials.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--225620950\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--2033075413\"\u003ePattern 3: Adding credentials to existing applications\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eOnce the attacker was able to impersonate a privileged Azure AD admin account, they added credentials to existing applications or service principals, usually with the permissions they wanted already associated and high traffic patterns (e.g. mail archival applications). There are some cases in which we see the attacker add permissions to existing applications or service principals. We also see cases in which a new application or service principal was set up for a short while and used to add the permissions to the existing applications or service principals, possibly to add a layer of indirection (e.g. using it to add a credential to another service principal, then deleting it).\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-583520139\"\u003eWhat we found\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAddition of federation trust relationships at the SP done which later resulted in SAML authentications of users with administrative privileges. The impersonated users later took actions consistent with attacker patterns described below.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eService Principals added into well known administrative roles such as Tenant Admin or Cloud Application Admin.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eReconnaissance to identify existing applications with application roles that have permissions to call Microsoft Graph.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe applications or service principals impersonated were different from customer to customer – the actor did not have a “go to” target.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe applications or service principals included both customer developer and vendor developed software.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eNo Microsoft 365 applications or service principals were used impersonated (customer credentials cannot be added to these applications and service principals).\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eToken forgeries consistent with pattern 1, above.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--1223934324\"\u003eWhat it implies\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAdministrative access to the Azure AD was gained.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAttacker did extensive recon to find unique applications which could be used to obfuscate their activity.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-1263578509\"\u003eWhat to look for\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAnomalous administrative session associated with modification of federation trust relationships.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eUnexpected service principals added to privileged roles in cloud environments.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-154285987\"\u003eWhat to do\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eReview all applications and service principals for credential modification activity.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eReview all applications and service principals for excess permissions.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eRemove all inactive service principals from your environment.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eRegularly roll creds for all applications and service principals.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--1782251195\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-705261638\"\u003ePattern 4: Queries impersonating existing applications\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eWith credentials added to an existing application or service principal, the actor proceeded to acquire an OAUTH access token for the application using the forged credentials, and call APIs with the permissions which had been assigned to that applications. Most of the API calls we detected were focused on email and document extraction, but in some cases API calls added users, or added permissions to other applications or service principals. Calls were generally very targeted, synchronizing then monitoring email for specific users.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--973110106\"\u003eWhat we found\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eApplication calls attempting to authenticated to Microsoft Graph resource with applicationID: \"00000003-0000-0000-c000-000000000000\"\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eImpersonated calls to the Microsoft Graph Mail.Read and Mail.ReadWrite endpoints.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eImpersonating calls came from anomalous endpoints. These endpoints were not repeated from customer to customer. The endpoints were usually Virtual Private Server (VPS) vendors.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-1514402727\"\u003eWhat it implies\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAttacker was primarily interested in persistence and reconnaissance.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAttacker was attempting to obfuscate their activity.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--293051736\"\u003eWhat to look for\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAnomalous requests to your resources from trusted applications or service principals.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eRequests from service principals that added or modified groups, users, applications, service principals, or trust relationships.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--2100506199\"\u003eWhat to do\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eReview all federation trust relationships, ensure all are valid.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eDetermine mechanism of administrative account impersonation (see below).\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eRoll administrative account credentials.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH1 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-128841196\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H1\u003e\n\u003cH1 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--1678613267\"\u003eOther Observations\u003c/H1\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThis section relates other observations of attacker behavior.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-1067065004\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--42227518\"\u003eAttacker access to on premises resources\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eOur optics into on premises behavior are limited, but here are the indicators we have as to how on premises access was gained. We recommend using on premises tools like \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/defender-for-identity/what-is\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eMicrosoft Defender for Identity\u003c/A\u003e (formerly Azure ATP) to detect other anomalies:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eCompromised network management software used as command and control to place malicious binaries which exfiltrated SAML token signing certificate.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eCompromised vendor credentials with existing administrative access (vendor network compromised).\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eCompromised service account credentials associated with compromised vendor software.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eUse of non-MFA service account.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--1849681981\"\u003eAttacker access to cloud resources\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eFor administrative access to the Microsoft 365 cloud, we observed:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eForged SAML tokens impersonating accounts with cloud administrative privileges.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAccounts without MFA required – these are easily compromised (see \u003cA href=\"https://aka.ms/yourpassworddoesntmatter\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003ehttps://aka.ms/yourpassworddoesntmatter\u003c/A\u003e)\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAccess from trusted vendor accounts where the attacker had compromised the vendor environment.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH1 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-379665414\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H1\u003e\n\u003cH1 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--1427789049\"\u003eIdentity Attack Graph\u003c/H1\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThis graph summarizes the vectors and combinations tracked in this document.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cDIV id=\"tinyMceEditorAlex Weinert_2\" class=\"mceNonEditable lia-copypaste-placeholder\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/DIV\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cspan class=\"lia-media-object lia-media-is-inline lia-media-size-large\" data-image-alt=\"Graph.png\"\u003e\u003cbutton class=\"lia-media-unstyled-btn\" type=\"button\" aria-haspopup=\"true\" aria-label=\"Enlarge Image Graph.png\"\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0yMDA3NjEwLTI0MjE0M2k4QTMzMzZEMzZEQkMyREM2?image-dimensions=832x400\u0026amp;revision=16\" width=\"832\" height=\"400\" alt=\"Graph.png\" /\u003e\u003c/button\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH1 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-1059723784\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H1\u003e\n\u003cH1 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--747730679\"\u003eOther Guidance\u003c/H1\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe following guidance may assist customers in protecting their environments.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eWe published an\u003cA href=\"https://aka.ms/solorigateazureadworkbook\" target=\"_self\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003e Azure AD Workbook\u003c/A\u003e to help you hunt for the behaviors described in this document.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTo protect Microsoft 365 resources from a compromise of on-premises environments: \u003cA href=\"https://aka.ms/protectm365\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003ehttps://aka.ms/protectm365\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eIf your organization has been compromised, review recovery guidance from DART at \u003cA href=\"https://aka.ms/dartrecoveryguide\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003ehttps://aka.ms/dartrecoveryguide\u003c/A\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTo configure for Zero Trust to increase explicit request verification, least privileged access, and assumed breach protection: \u003cA href=\"https://aka.ms/ztguide\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003ehttps://aka.ms/ztguide\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH1 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId-1739782154\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H1\u003e\n\u003cH1 id=\"community-2007610-toc-hId--67672309\"\u003eStay up to date\u003c/H1\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe Solarwinds attack is an ongoing investigation, and our teams continue to act as first responders to these attacks. As new information becomes available, we will make updates through our Microsoft Security Response Center (MSRC) blog at\u0026nbsp;\u003cA href=\"https://nam06.safelinks.protection.outlook.com/?url=https%3A%2F%2Fcomm.microsoft.com%2FPoliteMail245%2Fdefault.aspx%3Fpage%3DmOgDA41300uL7Q32_8nsGA%26ref_id%3DKI8WohcEM0KIdOtHZj3pQw\u0026amp;data=04%7C01%7Cadhall%40microsoft.com%7Ca48f35f285d94ccd698f08d8a3956502%7C72f988bf86f141af91ab2d7cd011db47%7C1%7C0%7C637439209352849256%7CUnknown%7CTWFpbGZsb3d8eyJWIjoiMC4wLjAwMDAiLCJQIjoiV2luMzIiLCJBTiI6Ik1haWwiLCJXVCI6Mn0%3D%7C1000\u0026amp;sdata=Xebz37S3gWRu3E8WvV1FRMz6vDhcBRDY0uQCZi9ogEc%3D\u0026amp;reserved=0\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener nofollow noreferrer\"\u003ehttps://aka.ms/solorigate\u003c/A\u003e.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e","body@stringLength":"26521","rawBody":"\u003cP\u003eMicrosoft recently \u003cA href=\"https://blogs.microsoft.com/on-the-issues/2020/12/13/customers-protect-nation-state-cyberattacks/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003edisclosed a set of complex techniques used by an advanced actor\u003c/A\u003e to execute attacks against several key customers. While we detected anomalies by analyzing requests from customer environments to the Microsoft 365 cloud, the attacks generalize to any Identity Provider or Service provider. For this reason, we want to detail what we know about these attacks from an Identity specific lens, so Identity vendors, IAAS, PAAS, and SAAS vendors (and organizations who use these products) can \u0026nbsp;detect them, remediate them, and most importantly, prevent them. This document is broadly intended to support the security teams who are focused on protection, hunting, and remediation in Identity vendors in the interest of protecting our mutual customers. It generally assumes an advanced understanding of identity infrastructure and related components.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eAs part of our ongoing security processes, we leverage threat intelligence and monitor for new indicators that could signal attacker activity. There are two anomalies pertinent to this report, and discussed at \u003cA href=\"https://aka.ms/solorigate\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003ehttps://aka.ms/solorigate\u003c/A\u003e:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cOL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAnomalies in SAML tokens being presented for access. In some impacted tenants, we detected anomalous SAML tokens - signed with customer certificates - being presented for access to the Microsoft Cloud. The anomalies indicate that the customer SAML token signing certificates may have been compromised, and that an attacker could be forging SAML tokens to access any resources that trust those certificates. Because compromise of a SAML token signing certificate typically requires administrative access, there is a high probability the presence of forged tokens implies customer on premises infrastructure may be compromised. Because the signing certificate is the root of trust for the federated trust relationship, it is unlikely Service Providers would detect the forgeries.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cSPAN\u003eAnomalies in Microsoft 365 API access patterns in a tenant. We have detected such anomalies in some impacted tenants which originate from an existing applications and service principals. This pattern indicates that an attacker with administrative credentials – possibly related to the SAML issues above - has added their own credentials to existing applications and service principals. The Microsoft 365 application program interfaces (APIs) can be used to access email, documents, chats, and configuration settings, such as email forwarding. Because highly privileged Azure AD administrative accounts are required to add credentials to service principals, these changes imply that one or more such privileged accounts have been compromised and may have made other significant changes within the impacted tenant.\u003c/SPAN\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/OL\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eBuilt-in security and monitoring capabilities provided by the Microsoft Cloud detected these anomalies, but such anomalies could present at any Identity Provider or Resource Provider, regardless of vendor.\u0026nbsp; Any resource which trusts a customer’s compromised SAML token signing certificate should be considered at risk.\u0026nbsp;The SAML attack is not specific to any particular identity system or identity vendor you use. It impacts any vendor’s on-premises or cloud identity system, and any resources that depend on industry-standard SAML identity federation. Likewise, while the specific mechanism for impersonating applications may vary from vendor to vendor, the pattern is vendor independent. We are not aware of any Microsoft software or cloud service vulnerability that has led to the exposure of customer SAML token signing certificates, the change in API usage, addition of credentials to service principals, or exposure of administrative credentials on premises or in the cloud. The traffic detected did not impact our production cloud environment\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eElements of the activity we have detected and discuss in this blog suggest it was orchestrated by a sophisticated attacker. We believe over time this activity may be determined to be state-sponsored, but we do not have sufficient evidence to support that conclusion at this time.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eIt is important to emphasize – these attacks were criminal in nature, and so sophisticated that even top security companies fell victim to them. The fault lies with the criminal actor, not the victims. Further, these attacks leveraged broadly used patterns that impact many levels of the IT industry – focusing on any one technology type or vendor limits our ability to see systemic problems. By studying them and learning from these broad issues together, we can improve security throughout the industry.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eWe are sharing this information to help vendors who provide Identity Providers and Service Provider software and services, as well as their customers, hunt for activity and better defend against similar attacks. We will update this information as our investigations evolve.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003eFirst things first – understanding typical environments\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003eIdentity experts may skip this section, but it may help level set terminology and understanding of key components of the attack.\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe following describes a typical environment. However, several caveats are worth mentioning:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cOL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eMost customers run on premises identity infrastructure. Microsoft offers popular versions of all such infrastructure, but several other vendors offer similar infrastructure.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eNot all components of this infrastructure are present in all environments.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe sophisticated actor adapted their attack to specific environments.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/OL\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eFor these reasons, it is important that you consider how elements of the attack pattern can impact your organization, even if your organization varies from the typical elements described below.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eComponents pertinent to this attack typically include:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eA \u003cU\u003eSAML Identity Provider (IDP)\u003c/U\u003e, which brokers authentication requests from applications to that directory. Applications and other identity providers – called Service Providers, or SPs – refer clients to federation servers to acquire access tokens which are signed by the SAML token signing certificate.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cU\u003eSAML Service Providers (SP)\u003c/U\u003e, also called Relying Parties, which are applications told to trust the tokens coming from a federation server, and which accept claims made in SAML tokens because they are signed by the trusted federation server’s SAML token signing certificate.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cU\u003eCloud Identity Providers (Cloud IDPs)\u003c/U\u003e, which would typically act as a Service Provider to the SAML Identity Provider but acts as an identity provider to applications which have been told to use it. (This is called Identity Provider chaining – where one Identity provider delegates authentication to another if so configured). Azure AD is an example of a Cloud IDP.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cU\u003eCloud Service Providers (Cloud SPs)\u003c/U\u003e, which rely on the tokens issued by a cloud IDP to validate requests.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cU\u003eApplications\u003c/U\u003e and \u003cU\u003eService Principals\u003c/U\u003e in Azure AD are two types of code that authenticate to Azure AD using certificates, keys, or passwords and to other applications or APIs using OAuth 2.0 access tokens granted Azure AD in order to talk to other applications, including Microsoft 365 Graph APIs.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cDIV id=\"tinyMceEditorAlex Weinert_0\" class=\"mceNonEditable lia-copypaste-placeholder\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/DIV\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cLI-IMAGE width=\"672\" height=\"277\" alt=\"Components.png\" align=\"inline\" id=\"242154iB86B0BCFEE13E907\" size=\"large\" sourcetype=\"new\" src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0yMDA3NjEwLTI0MjE1NGlCODZCMEJDRkVFMTNFOTA3?image-dimensions=750x750\u0026amp;revision=16\"\u003eTypical Components\u003c/LI-IMAGE\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eIf this is new to you, you can think of tokens as a ticket to a show, the SP as the ticket taker, and the IDP as the box office, and the signature as the little holograph on the ticket. If someone wants to go to the show, they go to the IDP, get a token, then present it to the SP who validates the holograph then lets them into the show.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eIn terms of a typical email interaction with Microsoft 365 with federated auth, think in terms of a user going to their on premises federation server to get a signed token, then presenting that token to Microsoft 365 (via Azure AD as a chained IDP) to get access after the signature is validated. If an application is trying to access that data, it will be pre-configured to know the IDP location, so it will always come to Azure AD first to get the token, but otherwise the OAUTH flows are similar.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eFor SAML federation relationships where Azure AD has been configured to trust a tenant-configured SAML token signing certificate from a customer-configured federation server, the federation server is the Identity Provider (IDP)and Azure AD is the Service Provider (SP).\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eApplications and Service Principals in Azure AD don’t use SAML or delegate trust to on premises federation servers; credentials must be managed directly in Azure AD.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003eAttacker Patterns\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eOK, let’s lay all of this out in terms of the typical attack patterns, and how you might go about looking for them. The actions were targeted in nature and varied from target to target. Not all indicators of compromise or methodologies are present in all cases.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eA rough overview of an indicative attack is as follows (see also \u003cA href=\"https://aka.ms/solorigate\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003ehttps://aka.ms/solorigate\u003c/A\u003e). Each attack varied in details, but this pattern emerged repeatedly:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe attacker compromises network management vendor software which is typically deployed with high privileges in on premises networks.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe attacker uses the on premises administrative access to extract SAML Token Signing certificate.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe attacker uses the stolen SAML Token Signing certificate to forge SAML tokens representing privileged cloud users.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe attacker uses the forged token to sign in impersonating the privileged user and add credentials to an existing application or service principal.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe attacker uses the added credentials to impersonate the existing application or service principal, and call Microsoft 365 APIs to extract mail.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cDIV id=\"tinyMceEditorAlex Weinert_1\" class=\"mceNonEditable lia-copypaste-placeholder\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/DIV\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cLI-IMAGE width=\"832\" height=\"481\" alt=\"Attack overview.png\" align=\"inline\" id=\"242156iDEE88BFBB6FC309D\" size=\"large\" sourcetype=\"new\" src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0yMDA3NjEwLTI0MjE1NmlERUU4OEJGQkI2RkMzMDlE?image-dimensions=750x750\u0026amp;revision=16\"\u003eAttacker Patterns\u003c/LI-IMAGE\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe details of each phase of the attack are described below.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003ePattern 1: Forged SAML tokens using Stolen SAML Token Signing Material\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe first significant pattern we have seen is evidence of forged SAML tokens. It is worth noting that we don’t retain logs for very long. We do this in accordance with our data retention and privacy policies. It varies by agreement, but is generally is 30 days, and we never log complete tokens, so we can’t see every aspect of a SAML token. Customers who want longer retention are encouraged to configure storage in Azure Monitor or other systems. What token anomalies we did detect were tokens which were anomalous in lifetime, usage location, or claims (particularly MFA claims). The anomalies were sufficient to convince us that the tokens were forged. We did not find this pattern in all cases.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat we found\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTokens with expiration instant exactly 3600 seconds or 144000 seconds (no milliseconds value) – note 144000 (40 hours) is exceptionally long\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTokens which were received at the same time as the issuance time– no latency between creation and usage\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTokens which were received BEFORE the issuance time – falsified issuance time after token received\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTokens used from outside normal user locations\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTokens which contained claims which were not previously seen from the tenant’s federation server\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTokens indicating MFA used when token claimed auth from within corporate boundary where MFA not required\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat it implies\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eSAML token signing certificate was exfiltrated from the customer environment and used to forge tokens by the actor.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAdministrative access to SAML Token Signing Certificate storage compromised, either via service administrative access or by direct device storage or memory inspection.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eDeep penetration of the customer environment, with administrative access to identity infrastructure or the hardware environment on which it executes.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat to look for\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eSAML Tokens received by the SP with configurations which deviate from the IDP’s configured behavior.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eSAML Tokens received by the SP without corresponding issuing logs at the IDP.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eSAML Tokens received by the SP with MFA claims but without corresponding MFA activity logs at the IDP.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eSAML Tokens which are received from IP addresses, agents, times, or for services which are anomalous for the requesting identity represented in the token.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eEvidence of unauthorized administrative activity.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat to do\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eDetermine mechanism of certificate exfiltration and remediate (see below)\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eRoll all SAML token signing certificates\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eConsider reducing your reliance on prem SAML trust where possible\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eConsider using an HSM to manage your SAML Token Signing Certificates\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003ePattern 2: Illegitimate registrations of SAML Trust Relationships\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eIn some cases, the SAML token forgeries described above correspond to configuration changes in the Service Provider. By impersonating a user with valid administrative credentials, the actor can change the configuration of the SAML Service Provider (in our case, Azure AD). In this case, the actor tells Azure AD to trust their certificate by, in effect, saying to the SP “There’s another SAML IDP you should trust, validate it with this public key.”\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat we found\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAddition of federation trust relationships at the SP done which later resulted in SAML authentications of users with administrative privileges. The attacker took care to follow naming conventions of or impersonate existing federation server names (e.g. FED_SERVER01 exists, and they add FED_SERVERO1). The impersonated users later took actions consistent with attacker patterns described below.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eToken forgeries consistent with pattern 1, above.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThese calls came from different IP addresses for each call and user, but generally tracked back to anonymizing VPN servers.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat it implies\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAdministrative access to the Azure AD was gained.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAttacker may have been unable to gain a toe-hold on premises, or was experimenting with other persistence mechanisms.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAttacker may have been unable to exfiltrate tokens, possibly due to use of HSM.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat to look for\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAnomalous administrative session associated with modification of federation trust relationships.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat to do\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eReview all federation trust relationships, ensure all are valid.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eDetermine mechanism of administrative account impersonation (see below).\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eRoll administrative account credentials.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003ePattern 3: Adding credentials to existing applications\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eOnce the attacker was able to impersonate a privileged Azure AD admin account, they added credentials to existing applications or service principals, usually with the permissions they wanted already associated and high traffic patterns (e.g. mail archival applications). There are some cases in which we see the attacker add permissions to existing applications or service principals. We also see cases in which a new application or service principal was set up for a short while and used to add the permissions to the existing applications or service principals, possibly to add a layer of indirection (e.g. using it to add a credential to another service principal, then deleting it).\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat we found\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAddition of federation trust relationships at the SP done which later resulted in SAML authentications of users with administrative privileges. The impersonated users later took actions consistent with attacker patterns described below.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eService Principals added into well known administrative roles such as Tenant Admin or Cloud Application Admin.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eReconnaissance to identify existing applications with application roles that have permissions to call Microsoft Graph.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe applications or service principals impersonated were different from customer to customer – the actor did not have a “go to” target.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eThe applications or service principals included both customer developer and vendor developed software.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eNo Microsoft 365 applications or service principals were used impersonated (customer credentials cannot be added to these applications and service principals).\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eToken forgeries consistent with pattern 1, above.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat it implies\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAdministrative access to the Azure AD was gained.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAttacker did extensive recon to find unique applications which could be used to obfuscate their activity.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat to look for\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAnomalous administrative session associated with modification of federation trust relationships.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eUnexpected service principals added to privileged roles in cloud environments.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat to do\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eReview all applications and service principals for credential modification activity.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eReview all applications and service principals for excess permissions.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eRemove all inactive service principals from your environment.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eRegularly roll creds for all applications and service principals.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003ePattern 4: Queries impersonating existing applications\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eWith credentials added to an existing application or service principal, the actor proceeded to acquire an OAUTH access token for the application using the forged credentials, and call APIs with the permissions which had been assigned to that applications. Most of the API calls we detected were focused on email and document extraction, but in some cases API calls added users, or added permissions to other applications or service principals. Calls were generally very targeted, synchronizing then monitoring email for specific users.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat we found\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eApplication calls attempting to authenticated to Microsoft Graph resource with applicationID: \"00000003-0000-0000-c000-000000000000\"\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eImpersonated calls to the Microsoft Graph Mail.Read and Mail.ReadWrite endpoints.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eImpersonating calls came from anomalous endpoints. These endpoints were not repeated from customer to customer. The endpoints were usually Virtual Private Server (VPS) vendors.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat it implies\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAttacker was primarily interested in persistence and reconnaissance.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAttacker was attempting to obfuscate their activity.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat to look for\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAnomalous requests to your resources from trusted applications or service principals.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eRequests from service principals that added or modified groups, users, applications, service principals, or trust relationships.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eWhat to do\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eReview all federation trust relationships, ensure all are valid.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eDetermine mechanism of administrative account impersonation (see below).\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eRoll administrative account credentials.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH1\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H1\u003e\n\u003cH1\u003eOther Observations\u003c/H1\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThis section relates other observations of attacker behavior.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eAttacker access to on premises resources\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eOur optics into on premises behavior are limited, but here are the indicators we have as to how on premises access was gained. We recommend using on premises tools like \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/defender-for-identity/what-is\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eMicrosoft Defender for Identity\u003c/A\u003e (formerly Azure ATP) to detect other anomalies:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eCompromised network management software used as command and control to place malicious binaries which exfiltrated SAML token signing certificate.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eCompromised vendor credentials with existing administrative access (vendor network compromised).\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eCompromised service account credentials associated with compromised vendor software.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eUse of non-MFA service account.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eAttacker access to cloud resources\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eFor administrative access to the Microsoft 365 cloud, we observed:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eForged SAML tokens impersonating accounts with cloud administrative privileges.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAccounts without MFA required – these are easily compromised (see \u003cA href=\"https://aka.ms/yourpassworddoesntmatter\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003ehttps://aka.ms/yourpassworddoesntmatter\u003c/A\u003e)\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eAccess from trusted vendor accounts where the attacker had compromised the vendor environment.\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cH1\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/H1\u003e\n\u003cH1\u003eIdentity Attack Graph\u003c/H1\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThis graph summarizes the vectors and combinations tracked in this document.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cDIV id=\"tinyMceEditorAlex Weinert_2\" class=\"mceNonEditable
